**Local source files are not present.** Your workspace does not contain source code. You **MUST** use Sourcegraph MCP tools to discover, read, and understand code before making any changes.

**Target Repositories (version-pinned mirrors):**

Use the repo name as the `repo` parameter for read_file/go_to_definition/find_references.


## Required Workflow

1. **Search first** — Use MCP tools to find relevant files and understand existing patterns
2. **Read remotely** — Use `sg_read_file` to read full file contents from Sourcegraph
3. **Edit locally** — Use Edit, Write, and Bash to create or modify files in your working directory
4. **Verify locally** — Run tests with Bash to check your changes

## Tool Selection

| Goal | Tool |
|------|------|
| Exact symbol/string | `sg_keyword_search` |
| Concepts/semantic search | `sg_nls_search` |
| Trace usage/callers | `sg_find_references` |

| Compare versions | `sg_compare_revisions` |

**Decision logic:**
1. Know the exact symbol? → `sg_keyword_search`
2. Know the concept, not the name? → `sg_nls_search`
3. Need definition of a symbol? → `sg_go_to_definition`
4. Need all callers/references? → `sg_find_references`
5. Need full file content? → `sg_read_file`

## Scoping (Always Do This)

```
repo:^github.com/ORG/REPO$           # Exact repo (preferred)
repo:github.com/ORG/                 # All repos in org
file:.*\.ts$                         # TypeScript only
file:src/api/                        # Specific directory
```

Start narrow. Expand only if results are empty.

## Efficiency Rules

- Chain searches logically: search → read → references → definition
- Don't re-search for the same pattern; use results from prior calls
- Prefer `sg_keyword_search` over `sg_nls_search` when you have exact terms
- Read 2-3 related files before synthesising, rather than one at a time
- Don't read 20+ remote files without writing code — once you understand the pattern, start implementing

## If Stuck

If MCP search returns no results:
1. Broaden the search query (synonyms, partial identifiers)
2. Try `sg_nls_search` for semantic matching
3. Use `sg_list_files` to browse the directory structure
4. Use `sg_list_repos` to verify the repository name

# Task: Kubernetes client-go Systems Guide

**Repository:** github.com/sg-evals/kubernetes--8c9c67c0 (mirror of kubernetes/kubernetes) (stripped snapshot)
**Output:** Write your document to `/workspace/documentation.md`

## Objective

Produce an advanced systems guide for client-go that explains API access, controller loops, and extension paths.

## Scope

Focus on the client-go subsystem.
Your document must explain component responsibilities, end-to-end flow, and extension or operational tradeoffs.

## Content Expectations

Address all of the following in your own structure:
- subsystem purpose, boundaries, and upstream/downstream dependencies
- key components and how responsibilities are split
- end-to-end control/request flow with concrete repository evidence
- failure modes, limits, and design tradeoffs
- extension points, customization hooks, and associated risks
- a concise map of the most important files/directories used in your analysis

## Quality Bar

- Cite concrete file paths from the repository.
- Explain behavior and interactions, not just API signatures.
- Include at least one section on operational implications (performance, reliability, or maintainability).
- Do not fabricate classes, functions, or files.

## Anti-Requirements

- Do not output `doc.go`.
- Do not provide shallow bullet dumps without system flow explanation.
- Do not rely on external docs not present in the workspace.

{"content":"1: # `client-go` Architecture\n2: \n3: This document explains the internal architecture of `client-go` for contributors. It describes the\n4: major components, how they interact, and the key design decisions that shape the library.\n5: \n6: ## Client Configuration\n7: \n8: There is an architectural separation between loading client configuration and using it. The\n9: `rest.Config` object is the in-memory representation of this configuration. The\n10: `tools/clientcmd` package is the standard factory for producing it. `clientcmd` handles the\n11: complex logic of parsing `kubeconfig` files, merging contexts, and handling external\n12: authentication providers (e.g., OIDC).\n13: \n14: ## REST Client\n15: \n16: The `rest.Client` is the foundational HTTP client that underpins all other clients. It separates\n17: the low-level concerns of HTTP transport, serialization, and error handling from higher-level,\n18: Kubernetes-specific object logic.\n19: \n20: The `rest.Config` object is used to build the underlying HTTP transport, which is typically a\n21: chain of `http.RoundTripper` objects. Each element in the chain is responsible for a specific\n22: task, such as adding an `Authorization` header. This is the mechanism by which all authentication\n23: is injected into requests.\n24: \n25: The client uses a builder pattern for requests (e.g., `.Verb()`, `.Resource()`), deferring\n26: response processing until a method like `.Into(&pod)` is called. 81-assistanttext-
The documentation has been written to `/workspace/documentation.md`. Here is a summary of what it covers:

**Structure:**

1. **Subsystem Purpose and Boundaries** — what client-go owns vs. what lives in `k8s.io/api`, `k8s.io/apimachinery`, and the API server, plus its upstream and downstream dependency graph.

2. **Key Components** — organized into four tiers: (1) transport/config (`rest.Config`, `transport/transport.go`, `tools/clientcmd/`), (2) HTTP request layer (`RESTClient`, `Request`, retry logic), (3) client models (typed `Clientset`, `DynamicClient`, `DiscoveryClient`, `MetadataClient`), and (4) controller infrastructure (informers, work queues, leader election, SSA builders).

3. **End-to-End Flow** — two concrete traces: a single API GET request through the rate limiter → transport chain → codec, and the full informer/controller loop from `Reflector.ListAndWatchWithContext` → `DeltaFIFO` → `sharedIndexInformer` → `sharedProcessor` → work queue → reconcile loop, with file-level citations throughout.

4. **Failure Modes and Tradeoffs** — watch expiry/relist behavior, the `HasSynced()` vs. freshness distinction, work queue `Forget` pitfalls, client-side vs. APF rate limiting, resync cost, and the `ThreadSafeStore` mutation hazard.

5. **Extension Points** — `WrapTransport`, `TransformFunc`, `WatchErrorHandler`, custom indexers, per-handler `HasSynced()`, feature gates, leader election callbacks, and server-side apply builders — each with associated risks.

6. **Operational Implications** — informer cache memory mitigation strategies, TLS connection pool fragmentation, reconnect jitter to prevent thundering herds, resync period selection, and batch processing throughput.

7. **File Map** — a reference table of the 30 most important files and their roles.

  "content": "{\"content\":\"1: # `client-go` Architecture\\n2: \\n3: This document explains the internal architecture of `client-go` for contributors. It describes the\\n4: major components, how they interact, and the key design decisions that shape the library.\\n5: \\n6: ## Client Configuration\\n7: \\n8: There is an architectural separation between loading client configuration and using it. The\\n9: `rest.Config` object is the in-memory representation of this configuration. The\\n10: `tools/clientcmd` package is the standard factory for producing it. `clientcmd` handles the\\n11: complex logic of parsing `kubeconfig` files, merging contexts, and handling external\\n12: authentication providers (e.g., OIDC).\\n13: \\n14: ## REST Client\\n15: \\n16: The `rest.Client` is the foundational HTTP client that underpins all other clients. It separates\\n17: the low-level concerns of HTTP transport, serialization, and error handling from higher-level,\\n18: Kubernetes-specific object logic.\\n19: \\n20: The `rest.Config` object is used to build the underlying HTTP transport, which is typically a\\n21: chain of `http.RoundTripper` objects. Each element in the chain is responsible for a specific\\n22: task, such as adding an `Authorization` header. This is the mechanism by which all authentication\\n23: is injected into requests.\\n24: \\n25: The client uses a builder pattern for requests (e.g., `.Verb()`, `.Resource()`), deferring\\n26: response processing until a method like `.Into(&pod)` is called. This separation is key to\\n27: supporting different client models from a common base.\\n28: \\n29: ### Endpoint Interactions\\n30: \\n31: *   **Content Negotiation:** The client uses HTTP `Accept` headers to negotiate the wire format\\n32:     (JSON or Protobuf). A key performance optimization using this mechanism is the ability to\\n33:     request metadata-only objects via the `as=PartialObjectMetadata;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameter.\\n34:     Also the `as=Table;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameters may be used to request lists as tables. \\n35: *   **Subresources:** The client can target standard subresources like `/status` or `/scale` for\\n36:     object mutations, and it can also handle action-oriented subresources like `/logs` or\\n37:     `/exec`, which often involve streaming data.\\n38: *   **List Pagination:** For `LIST` requests, the client can specify a `limit`. The server will\\n39:     return up to that many items and, if more exist, a `continue` token. The client is\\n40:     responsible for passing this token in a subsequent request to retrieve the next page.\\n41:     Higher-level tools like the `Reflector`'s `ListerWatcher` handle this logic automatically.\\n42: *   **Streaming Watches:** A `WATCH` request returns a `watch.Interface` (from\\n43:     `k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/watch`), which provides a channel of structured `watch.Event`\\n44:     objects (`ADDED`, `MODIFIED`, `DELETED`, `BOOKMARK`). This decouples the watch consumer from\\n45:     the underlying streaming protocol.\\n46: \\n47: ### Errors, Warnings, and Rate Limiting\\n48: \\n49: *   **Structured Errors:** The client deserializes non-2xx responses into a structured\\n50:     `errors.StatusError`, enabling programmatic error handling (e.g., `errors.IsNotFound(err)`).\\n51: *   **Warnings:** It processes non-fatal `Warning` headers from the API server via a\\n52:     `WarningHandler`.\\n53: *   **Client-Side Rate Limiting:** The `QPS` and `Burst` settings in `rest.Config` are the\\n54:     client's half of the contract with the server's API Priority and Fairness system.\\n55: *   **Server-Side Throttling:** The client's default transport automatically handles HTTP `429`\\n56:     responses by reading the `Retry-After` header, waiting, and retrying the request.\\n57: \\n58: ## Typed and Dynamic Clients\\n59: \\n60: To handle the extensible nature of the Kubernetes API, `client-go` provides two primary client\\n61: models.\\n62: \\n63: The **`kubernetes.Clientset`** provides compile-time, type-safe access to core, built-in APIs.\\n64: \\n65: The **`dynamic.DynamicClient`** represents all objects as `unstructured.Unstructured`, allowing it\\n66: to interact with any API resource, including CRDs. It relies on two discovery mechanisms:\\n67: 1.  The **`discovery.DiscoveryClient`** determines *what* resources exist. The\\n68:     **`CachedDiscoveryClient`** is an optimization that caches this data on disk to solve.\\n69: 2.  The **OpenAPI schema** (fetched from `/openapi/v3`) describes the *structure* of those\\n70:     resources, providing the schema awareness needed by the dynamic client.\\n71: \\n72: ## Code Generation\\n73: \\n74: A core architectural principle of `client-go` is the use of code generation to provide a\\n75: strongly-typed, compile-time-safe interface for specific API GroupVersions. This makes\\n76: controller code more robust and easier to maintain. The tools in `k8s.io/code-generator` produce\\n77: several key components:\\n78: \\n79: *   **Typed Clientsets:** The primary interface for interacting with a specific GroupVersion.\\n80: *   **Typed Listers:** The read-only, cached accessors used by controllers.\\n81: *   **Typed Informers:** The machinery for populating the cache for a specific type.\\n82: *   **Apply Configurations:** The type-safe builders for Server-Side Apply.\\n83: \\n84: A contributor modifying a built-in API type **must** run the code generation scripts to update all\\n85: of these dependent components. For the Kubernetes project, `hack/update-codegen.sh` runs code generation.\\n86: \\n87: `sample-controller` shows how code generate can be configured to build custom controllers.\\n88: \\n89: ## Controller Infrastructure\\n90: \\n91: The `tools/cache` package provides the core infrastructure for controllers, replacing a high-load,\\n92: request-based pattern with a low-load, event-driven, cached model.\\n93: \\n94: The data flow is as follows:\\n95: \\n96: ```mermaid\\n97: graph TD\\n98:     subgraph \\\"Kubernetes API\\\"\\n99:         API_Server[API Server]\\n100:     end\\n101: \\n102:     subgraph \\\"client-go: Informer Mechanism\\\"\\n103:         Reflector(\\\"1. Reflector\\\")\\n104:         DeltaFIFO(\\\"2. DeltaFIFO\\\")\\n105:         Indexer[\\\"3. Indexer (Cache)\\\"]\\n106:         EventHandlers(\\\"4. Event Handlers\\\")\\n107:     end\\n108: \\n109:     subgraph \\\"User Code\\\"\\n110:         WorkQueue[\\\"5. Work Queue\\\"]\\n111:         Controller(\\\"6. Controller\\\")\\n112:     end\\n113: \\n114:     API_Server -- LIST/WATCH --> Reflector\\n115:     Reflector -- Puts changes into --> DeltaFIFO\\n116:     DeltaFIFO -- Is popped by internal loop, which updates --> Indexer\\n117:     Indexer -- Update triggers --> EventHandlers\\n118:     EventHandlers -- Adds key to --> WorkQueue\\n119:     WorkQueue -- Is processed by --> Controller\\n120:     Controller -- Reads from cache via Lister --> Indexer\\n121: ```\\n122: \\n123: A **`Reflector`** performs a `LIST` to get a consistent snapshot of a resource, identified by a\\n124: `resourceVersion`. It then starts a `WATCH` from that `resourceVersion` to receive a continuous\\n125: stream of subsequent changes. The `Reflector`'s relist/rewatch loop is designed to solve the\\n126: **\\\"too old\\\" `resourceVersion` error** by re-listing. To make this recovery more efficient, the\\n127: `Reflector` consumes **watch bookmarks** from the server, which provide a more recent\\n128: `resourceVersion` to restart from.\\n129: \\n130: The **`Lister`** is the primary, read-only, thread-safe interface for a controller's business\\n131: logic to access the `Indexer`'s cache.\\n132: \\n133: ## Controller Patterns\\n134: \\n135: The controller infrastructure is architecturally decoupled from the controller's business logic to\\n136: ensure resiliency.\\n137: \\n138: The **`util/workqueue`** creates a critical boundary between event detection (the informer's job)\\n139: and reconciliation (the controller's job). Informer event handlers only add an object's key to the\\n140: work queue. This allows the controller to retry failed operations with exponential backoff without\\n141: blocking the informer's watch stream.\\n142: \\n143: For high availability, the **`tools/leaderelection`** package provides the standard architectural\\n144: solution to ensure single-writer semantics by having replicas compete to acquire a lock on a\\n145: shared `Lease` object.\\n146: \\n147: ## Server-Side Apply\\n148: \\n149: `client-go` provides a distinct architectural pattern for object mutation that aligns with the\\n150: server's declarative model. This is a separate workflow from the traditional `get-modify-update`\\n151: model that allows multiple controllers to safely co-manage the same object. The\\n152: **`applyconfigurations`** package provides the generated, type-safe builder API used to\\n153: construct the declarative patch.\\n154: \\n155: ## Versioning and Compatibility\\n156: \\n157: `client-go` has a strict versioning relationship with the main Kubernetes repository. A `client-go`\\n158: version `v0.X.Y` corresponds to the Kubernetes version `v1.X.Y`.\\n159: \\n160: The Kubernetes API has strong backward compatibility guarantees: a client built with an older\\n161: version of `client-go` will work with a newer API server. However, the reverse is not guaranteed.\\n162: A contributor must not break compatibility with supported versions of the Kubernetes API server.\\n163: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/kubernetes--8c9c67c0@8c9c67c000104450cfc5a5f48053a9a84b73cf93/-/blob/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/ARCHITECTURE.md\"}",
  "structuredContent": {
    "content": "1: # `client-go` Architecture\n2: \n3: This document explains the internal architecture of `client-go` for contributors. It describes the\n4: major components, how they interact, and the key design decisions that shape the library.\n5: \n6: ## Client Configuration\n7: \n8: There is an architectural separation between loading client configuration and using it. The\n9: `rest.Config` object is the in-memory representation of this configuration. The\n10: `tools/clientcmd` package is the standard factory for producing it. `clientcmd` handles the\n11: complex logic of parsing `kubeconfig` files, merging contexts, and handling external\n12: authentication providers (e.g., OIDC).\n13: \n14: ## REST Client\n15: \n16: The `rest.Client` is the foundational HTTP client that underpins all other clients. It separates\n17: the low-level concerns of HTTP transport, serialization, and error handling from higher-level,\n18: Kubernetes-specific object logic.\n19: \n20: The `rest.Config` object is used to build the underlying HTTP transport, which is typically a\n21: chain of `http.RoundTripper` objects. Each element in the chain is responsible for a specific\n22: task, such as adding an `Authorization` header. This is the mechanism by which all authentication\n23: is injected into requests.\n24: \n25: The client uses a builder pattern for requests (e.g., `.Verb()`, `.Resource()`), deferring\n26: response processing until a method like `.Into(&pod)` is called. This separation is key to\n27: supporting different client models from a common base.\n28: \n29: ### Endpoint Interactions\n30: \n31: *   **Content Negotiation:** The client uses HTTP `Accept` headers to negotiate the wire format\n32:     (JSON or Protobuf). A key performance optimization using this mechanism is the ability to\n33:     request metadata-only objects via the `as=PartialObjectMetadata;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameter.\n34:     Also the `as=Table;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameters may be used to request lists as tables. \n35: *   **Subresources:** The client can target standard subresources like `/status` or `/scale` for\n36:     object mutations, and it can also handle action-oriented subresources like `/logs` or\n37:     `/exec`, which often involve streaming data.\n38: *   **List Pagination:** For `LIST` requests, the client can specify a `limit`. The server will\n39:     return up to that many items and, if more exist, a `continue` token. The client is\n40:     responsible for passing this token in a subsequent request to retrieve the next page.\n41:     Higher-level tools like the `Reflector`'s `ListerWatcher` handle this logic automatically.\n42: *   **Streaming Watches:** A `WATCH` request returns a `watch.Interface` (from\n43:     `k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/watch`), which provides a channel of structured `watch.Event`\n44:     objects (`ADDED`, `MODIFIED`, `DELETED`, `BOOKMARK`). This decouples the watch consumer from\n45:     the underlying streaming protocol.\n46: \n47: ### Errors, Warnings, and Rate Limiting\n48: \n49: *   **Structured Errors:** The client deserializes non-2xx responses into a structured\n50:     `errors.StatusError`, enabling programmatic error handling (e.g., `errors.IsNotFound(err)`).\n51: *   **Warnings:** It processes non-fatal `Warning` headers from the API server via a\n52:     `WarningHandler`.\n53: *   **Client-Side Rate Limiting:** The `QPS` and `Burst` settings in `rest.Config` are the\n54:     client's half of the contract with the server's API Priority and Fairness system.\n55: *   **Server-Side Throttling:** The client's default transport automatically handles HTTP `429`\n56:     responses by reading the `Retry-After` header, waiting, and retrying the request.\n57: \n58: ## Typed and Dynamic Clients\n59: \n60: To handle the extensible nature of the Kubernetes API, `client-go` provides two primary client\n61: models.\n62: \n63: The **`kubernetes.Clientset`** provides compile-time, type-safe access to core, built-in APIs.\n64: \n65: The **`dynamic.DynamicClient`** represents all objects as `unstructured.Unstructured`, allowing it\n66: to interact with any API resource, including CRDs. It relies on two discovery mechanisms:\n67: 1.  The **`discovery.DiscoveryClient`** determines *what* resources exist. The\n68:     **`CachedDiscoveryClient`** is an optimization that caches this data on disk to solve.\n69: 2.  The **OpenAPI schema** (fetched from `/openapi/v3`) describes the *structure* of those\n70:     resources, providing the schema awareness needed by the dynamic client.\n71: \n72: ## Code Generation\n73: \n74: A core architectural principle of `client-go` is the use of code generation to provide a\n75: strongly-typed, compile-time-safe interface for specific API GroupVersions. This makes\n76: controller code more robust and easier to maintain. The tools in `k8s.io/code-generator` produce\n77: several key components:\n78: \n79: *   **Typed Clientsets:** The primary interface for interacting with a specific GroupVersion.\n80: *   **Typed Listers:** The read-only, cached accessors used by controllers.\n81: *   **Typed Informers:** The machinery for populating the cache for a specific type.\n82: *   **Apply Configurations:** The type-safe builders for Server-Side Apply.\n83: \n84: A contributor modifying a built-in API type **must** run the code generation scripts to update all\n85: of these dependent components. For the Kubernetes project, `hack/update-codegen.sh` runs code generation.\n86: \n87: `sample-controller` shows how code generate can be configured to build custom controllers.\n88: \n89: ## Controller Infrastructure\n90: \n91: The `tools/cache` package provides the core infrastructure for controllers, replacing a high-load,\n92: request-based pattern with a low-load, event-driven, cached model.\n93: \n94: The data flow is as follows:\n95: \n96: ```mermaid\n97: graph TD\n98:     subgraph \"Kubernetes API\"\n99:         API_Server[API Server]\n100:     end\n101: \n102:     subgraph \"client-go: Informer Mechanism\"\n103:         Reflector(\"1. Reflector\")\n104:         DeltaFIFO(\"2. DeltaFIFO\")\n105:         Indexer[\"3. Indexer (Cache)\"]\n106:         EventHandlers(\"4. Event Handlers\")\n107:     end\n108: \n109:     subgraph \"User Code\"\n110:         WorkQueue[\"5. Work Queue\"]\n111:         Controller(\"6. Controller\")\n112:     end\n113: \n114:     API_Server -- LIST/WATCH --> Reflector\n115:     Reflector -- Puts changes into --> DeltaFIFO\n116:     DeltaFIFO -- Is popped by internal loop, which updates --> Indexer\n117:     Indexer -- Update triggers --> EventHandlers\n118:     EventHandlers -- Adds key to --> WorkQueue\n119:     WorkQueue -- Is processed by --> Controller\n120:     Controller -- Reads from cache via Lister --> Indexer\n121: ```\n122: \n123: A **`Reflector`** performs a `LIST` to get a consistent snapshot of a resource, identified by a\n124: `resourceVersion`. It then starts a `WATCH` from that `resourceVersion` to receive a continuous\n125: stream of subsequent changes. The `Reflector`'s relist/rewatch loop is designed to solve the\n126: **\"too old\" `resourceVersion` error** by re-listing. To make this recovery more efficient, the\n127: `Reflector` consumes **watch bookmarks** from the server, which provide a more recent\n128: `resourceVersion` to restart from.\n129: \n130: The **`Lister`** is the primary, read-only, thread-safe interface for a controller's business\n131: logic to access the `Indexer`'s cache.\n132: \n133: ## Controller Patterns\n134: \n135: The controller infrastructure is architecturally decoupled from the controller's business logic to\n136: ensure resiliency.\n137: \n138: The **`util/workqueue`** creates a critical boundary between event detection (the informer's job)\n139: and reconciliation (the controller's job). Informer event handlers only add an object's key to the\n140: work queue. This allows the controller to retry failed operations with exponential backoff without\n141: blocking the informer's watch stream.\n142: \n143: For high availability, the **`tools/leaderelection`** package provides the standard architectural\n144: solution to ensure single-writer semantics by having replicas compete to acquire a lock on a\n145: shared `Lease` object.\n146: \n147: ## Server-Side Apply\n148: \n149: `client-go` provides a distinct architectural pattern for object mutation that aligns with the\n150: server's declarative model. This is a separate workflow from the traditional `get-modify-update`\n151: model that allows multiple controllers to safely co-manage the same object. The\n152: **`applyconfigurations`** package provides the generated, type-safe builder API used to\n153: construct the declarative patch.\n154: \n155: ## Versioning and Compatibility\n156: \n157: `client-go` has a strict versioning relationship with the main Kubernetes repository. A `client-go`\n158: version `v0.X.Y` corresponds to the Kubernetes version `v1.X.Y`.\n159: \n160: The Kubernetes API has strong backward compatibility guarantees: a client built with an older\n161: version of `client-go` will work with a newer API server. However, the reverse is not guaranteed.\n162: A contributor must not break compatibility with supported versions of the Kubernetes API server.\n163: ",
    "url": "https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/kubernetes--8c9c67c0@8c9c67c000104450cfc5a5f48053a9a84b73cf93/-/blob/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/ARCHITECTURE.md"
  }

One\\n46: // SharedInformer provides linkage to objects of a particular API\\n47: // group and kind/resource.  The linked object collection of a\\n48: // SharedInformer may be further restricted to one namespace (if\\n49: // applicable) and/or by label selector and/or field selector.\\n50: //\\n51: // The authoritative state of an object is what apiservers provide\\n52: // access to, and an object goes through a strict sequence of states.\\n53: // An object state is either (1) present with a ResourceVersion and\\n54: // other appropriate content or (2) \\\"absent\\\".\\n55: //\\n56: // A SharedInformer maintains a local cache --- exposed by GetStore(),\\n57: // by GetIndexer() in the case of an indexed informer, and possibly by\\n58: // machinery involved in creating and/or accessing the informer --- of\\n59: // the state of each relevant object.  This cache is eventually\\n60: // consistent with the authoritative state.  This means that, unless\\n61: // prevented by persistent communication problems, if ever a\\n62: // particular object ID X is authoritatively associated with a state S\\n63: // then for every SharedInformer I whose collection includes (X, S)\\n64: // eventually either (1) I's cache associates X with S or a later\\n65: // state of X, (2) I is stopped, or (3) the authoritative state\\n66: // service for X terminates.  To be formally complete, we say that the\\n67: // absent state meets any restriction by label selector or field\\n68: // selector.\\n69: //\\n70: // For a given informer and relevant object ID X, the sequence of\\n71: // states that appears in the informer's cache is a subsequence of the\\n72: // states authoritatively associated with X.  That is, some states\\n73: // might never appear in the cache but ordering among the appearing\\n74: // states is correct.  Note, however, that there is no promise about\\n75: // ordering between states seen for different objects.\\n76: //\\n77: // The local cache starts out empty, and gets populated and updated\\n78: // during `Run()`.\\n79: //\\n80: // As a simple example, if a collection of objects is henceforth\\n81: // unchanging, a SharedInformer is created that links to that\\n82: // collection, and that SharedInformer is `Run()` then that\\n83: // SharedInformer's cache eventually holds an exact copy of that\\n84: // collection (unless it is stopped too soon, the authoritative state\\n85: // service ends, or communication problems between the two\\n86: // persistently thwart achievement).\\n87: //\\n88: // As another simple example, if the local cache ever holds a\\n89: // non-absent state for some object ID and the object is eventually\\n90: // removed from the authoritative state then eventually the object is\\n91: // removed from the local cache (unless the SharedInformer is stopped\\n92: // too soon, the authoritative state service ends, or communication\\n93: // problems persistently thwart the desired result).\\n94: //\\n95: // The keys in the Store are of the form namespace/name for namespaced\\n96: // objects, and are simply the name for non-namespaced objects.\\n97: // Clients can use `MetaNamespaceKeyFunc(obj)` to extract the key for\\n98: // a given object, and `SplitMetaNamespaceKey(key)` to split a key\\n99: // into its constituent parts.\\n100: //\\n101: // Every query against the local cache is answered entirely from one\\n102: // snapshot of the cache's state.  Thus, the result of a `List` call\\n103: // will not contain two entries with the same namespace and name.\\n104: //\\n105: // A client is identified here by a ResourceEventHandler. It is possible that additional cache\\n117: // and index updates happen before such a prescribed notification.\\n118: // For a given SharedInformer and client, the notifications are\\n119: // delivered sequentially. Clients that\\n127: // need to detect such cases might do so by comparing the `ObjectMeta.UID`\\n128: // field of the old and the new object in the code that handles update\\n129: // notifications (i.e. `OnUpdate` method of ResourceEventHandler).\\n130: //\\n131: // A client must process each notification promptly; a SharedInformer\\n132: // is not engineered to deal well with a large backlog of\\n133: // notifications to deliver. One use case is to strip unused\\n227: \\t// metadata fields out of objects to save on RAM cost.\\n228: \\t//\\n229: \\t// Must be set before starting the informer.\\n230: \\t//\\n231: \\t// Please see the comment on TransformFunc for more details.\\n232: \\tSetTransform(handler TransformFunc) error\\n233: \\n234: \\t// IsStopped reports whether the informer has already been stopped.\\n235: \\t// Adding event handlers to already stopped informers is not possible.\\n236: \\t// An informer already stopped will never be started again.\\n237: \\tIsStopped() bool\\n238: }\\n239: \\n240: // Opaque interface representing the registration of ResourceEventHandler for\\n241: // a SharedInformer.     "content": "120: // object ID, the notifications are delivered in order.  Because\n121: // `ObjectMeta.UID` has no role in identifying objects, it is possible\n122: // that when (1) object O1 with ID (e.g. namespace and name) X and\n123: // `ObjectMeta.UID` U1 in the SharedInformer's local cache is deleted\n124: // and later (2) another object O2 with ID X and ObjectMeta.UID U2 is\n125: // created the informer's clients are not notified of (1) and (2) but\n126: // rather are notified only of an update from O1 to O2. This implementation does not guarantee that only one\\n20: // client is acting as a leader (a.k.a. fencing).\\n21: //\\n22: // A client only acts on timestamps captured locally to infer the state of the\\n23: // leader election. The client does not consider timestamps in the leader\\n24: // election record to be accurate because these timestamps may not have been\\n25: // produced by a local clock. The implemention does not depend on their\\n26: // accuracy and only uses their change to indicate that another client has\\n27: // renewed the leader lease. Thus the implementation is tolerant to arbitrary\\n28: // clock skew, but is not tolerant to arbitrary clock skew rate.\\n29: //\\n30: // However the level of tolerance to skew rate can be configured by setting\\n31: // RenewDeadline and LeaseDuration appropriately. The tolerance expressed as a\\n32: // maximum tolerated ratio of time passed on the fastest node to time passed on\\n33: // the slowest node can be approximately achieved with a configuration that sets\\n34: // the same ratio of LeaseDuration to RenewDeadline. For example if a user wanted\\n35: // to tolerate some nodes progressing forward in time twice as fast as other nodes,\\n36: // the user could set LeaseDuration to 60 seconds and RenewDeadline to 30 seconds.\\n37: //\\n38: // While not required, some method of clock synchronization between nodes in the\\n39: // cluster is highly recommended. It's important to keep in mind when configuring\\n40: // this client that the tolerance to skew rate varies inversely to master\\n41: // availability.\\n42: //\\n43: // Larger clusters often have a more lenient SLA for API latency. This should be\\n44: // taken into account when configuring the client. The rate of leader transitions\\n45: // should be monitored and RetryPeriod and LeaseDuration should be increased\\n46: // until the rate is stable and acceptably low. It's important to keep in mind\\n47: // when configuring this client that the tolerance to API latency varies inversely\\n48: // to master availability.\\n49: //\\n50: // DISCLAIMER: this is an alpha API. This implementation does not guarantee that only one\n20: // client is acting as a leader (a.k.a. fencing).\n21: //\n22: // A client only acts on timestamps captured locally to infer the state of the\n23: // leader election. The client does not consider timestamps in the leader\n24: // election record to be accurate because these timestamps may not have been\n25: // produced by a local clock. The implemention does not depend on their\n26: // accuracy and only uses their change to indicate that another client has\n27: // renewed the leader lease. Thus the implementation is tolerant to arbitrary\n28: // clock skew, but is not tolerant to arbitrary clock skew rate.\n29: //\n30: // However the level of tolerance to skew rate can be configured by setting\n31: // RenewDeadline and LeaseDuration appropriately. The tolerance expressed as a\n32: // maximum tolerated ratio of time passed on the fastest node to time passed on\n33: // the slowest node can be approximately achieved with a configuration that sets\n34: // the same ratio of LeaseDuration to RenewDeadline. For example if a user wanted\n35: // to tolerate some nodes progressing forward in time twice as fast as other nodes,\n36: // the user could set LeaseDuration to 60 seconds and RenewDeadline to 30 seconds.\n37: //\n38: // While not required, some method of clock synchronization between nodes in the\n39: // cluster is highly recommended. It's important to keep in mind when configuring\n40: // this client that the tolerance to skew rate varies inversely to master\n41: // availability.\n42: //\n43: // Larger clusters often have a more lenient SLA for API latency. This should be\n44: // taken into account when configuring the client. The rate of leader transitions\n45: // should be monitored and RetryPeriod and LeaseDuration should be increased\n46: // until the rate is stable and acceptably low. It's important to keep in mind\n47: // when configuring this client that the tolerance to API latency varies inversely\n48: // to master availability.\n49: //\n50: // DISCLAIMER: this is an alpha API. {
  "content": "# client-go Advanced Systems Guide\n\n## 1. Subsystem Purpose, Boundaries, and Dependencies\n\n`client-go` is the official Go client library for the Kubernetes API. It lives in the Kubernetes monorepo under `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/` and is periodically published to a standalone module. Its version tag `v0.X.Y` directly tracks Kubernetes release `v1.X.Y`.\n\n**What client-go is responsible for:**\n- Providing typed, dynamic, and metadata-only access to the Kubernetes API server over HTTP/HTTPS.\n- Managing authentication credential loading and injection (kubeconfig, in-cluster service accounts, exec plugins, OIDC).\n- Implementing the informer/cache machinery that powers every controller in the Kubernetes control plane.\n- Offering high-level utilities: work queues, leader election, server-side apply builders, and event recording.\n\n**What it explicitly does not own:**\n- API type definitions live in `k8s.io/api` and `k8s.io/apimachinery`.\n- Code-generation tooling lives in `k8s.io/code-generator`.\n- The API server's watch cache, etcd, and any server-side logic.\n\n**Upstream dependencies** (consumed by client-go):\n- `k8s.io/apimachinery` \u2013 runtime, schema, serialization, watch primitives.\n- `k8s.io/api` \u2013 concrete typed API objects (Pod, Deployment, etc.).\n- `golang.org/x/time/rate` \u2013 token-bucket rate limiter used in work queues.\n\n**Downstream consumers** (built on top of client-go):\n- Every Kubernetes built-in controller (`kube-controller-manager`).\n- The Kubernetes scheduler.\n- Third-party operators and controllers via `controller-runtime`.\n- `kubectl` (uses client-go's `rest`, `dynamic`, and `discovery` packages directly).\n\n---\n\n## 2. Key Components and Responsibility Split\n\nThe library divides into four logical tiers:\n\n### Tier 1: Transport and Configuration\n\n**`rest/config.go` \u2013 `rest.Config`**\nThe in-memory representation of all connection parameters: API server host, TLS settings, authentication credentials, QPS/burst limits, and custom `http.RoundTripper` chains. The defaults (`DefaultQPS = 5.0`, `DefaultBurst = 10`) are defined here and are deliberately conservative.\n\n**`transport/transport.go` \u2013 transport layer**\n`transport.New(config)` produces the final `http.RoundTripper`. It constructs a base TLS transport (retrieved from a process-wide `tlsCache` to avoid redundant TLS handshakes) and then wraps it with `HTTPWrappersForConfig`, injecting authentication (bearer tokens, client certificates, exec plugin tokens) and other per-request decorators as a middleware chain.\n\n**`tools/clientcmd/` \u2013 kubeconfig parsing**\nResponsible for loading, merging, and resolving `kubeconfig` files. It converts the on-disk representation into a `rest.Config`. This is the only package responsible for kubeconfig's complex precedence rules (file flags, `KUBECONFIG` env, `~/.kube/config`).\n\n### Tier 2: HTTP Request Layer\n\n**`rest/client.go` \u2013 `RESTClient`**\nThe lowest-level HTTP client. Built from a `rest.Config`, it exposes a builder pattern: `.Verb(\"GET\").Resource(\"pods\").Namespace(\"default\").Name(\"nginx\")`. It knows about Kubernetes URL conventions and content negotiation (JSON vs. Protobuf vs. CBOR).\n\n**`rest/request.go` \u2013 `Request`**\nAssembles the final HTTP request. It holds all path segments, query parameters, and headers. Execution is deferred until a terminal method is called (`.Do(ctx)`, `.DoRaw(ctx)`, `.Stream(ctx)`, `.Watch(ctx)`). Rate limiting is enforced here via `flowcontrol.RateLimiter` before the HTTP call is issued.\n\n**`rest/with_retry.go` \u2013 retry logic**\nEncapsulates retry decisions. The `WithRetry` interface's `IsNextRetry` method handles 429 (reads `Retry-After`) and 5xx responses. The number of retries is configurable; the default is 10 for `Watch` and 0 for mutating verbs to avoid accidental double-writes.\n\n### Tier 3: Client Models\n\n**`kubernetes/` \u2013 typed `Clientset`**\nGenerated code. Provides compile-time-safe access to built-in GroupVersionResources. The entry point `kubernetes.NewForConfig(cfg)` produces a `Clientset` aggregating group-versioned clients (e.g., `clientset.CoreV1().Pods(\"default\").Get(...)`). Internally each typed client calls `RESTClient`.\n\n**`dynamic/simple.go` \u2013 `DynamicClient`**\nRepresents every object as `unstructured.Unstructured`. Used by operators handling CRDs at runtime without generated code. Every call requires a `schema.GroupVersionResource` to be provided by the caller.\n\n**`discovery/` \u2013 `DiscoveryClient`**\nInterrogates `/api`, `/apis`, and `/openapi/v3` to determine which resources exist and their capabilities. The `CachedDiscoveryClient` writes this data to disk to avoid re-querying on every process start\u2014important for controllers that call `restmapper.GetAPIGroupResources` during startup.\n\n**`metadata/` \u2013 `MetadataClient`**\nA specialized client that requests objects using the `Accept: application/json;as=PartialObjectMetadata` header, instructing the API server to return only `ObjectMeta`. Used by garbage collection and other controllers that do not need the full object body.\n\n### Tier 4: Controller Infrastructure\n\n**`tools/cache/` \u2013 informer machinery**\nThe heart of the controller model. See Section 3 for the full flow.\n\n**`util/workqueue/` \u2013 work queues**\nProvides `TypedInterface[T]`, `TypedDelayingInterface[T]`, and `TypedRateLimitingInterface[T]` \u2014 a layered set of queues used to decouple event detection from reconciliation.\n\n**`tools/leaderelection/` \u2013 leader election**\nEnables high-availability controllers by competing for a `Lease` object on the API server.\n\n**`applyconfigurations/` \u2013 server-side apply builders**\nGenerated type-safe structs that express the desired state as a partial object, used with the `Apply` verb.\n\n---\n\n## 3. End-to-End Control Flow\n\n### 3a. Single API Request\n\n1. **Client code** calls `clientset.CoreV1().Pods(\"default\").Get(ctx, \"nginx\", metav1.GetOptions{})`.\n2. This calls into the generated typed client, which calls `r.c.Get()` on the underlying `RESTClient`.\n3. `RESTClient` constructs a `Request` (in `rest/request.go:Request`). The path is built as `/api/v1/namespaces/default/pods/nginx`.\n4. **Rate limiting**: `Request.tryThrottle()` calls `rateLimiter.Accept()`, blocking if the client is above its configured QPS. Throttle events above `longThrottleLatency` (50ms) are logged.\n5. **HTTP execution**: `http.Client.Do()` is called with the assembled `*http.Request`. The transport chain executes: TLS \u2192 auth token injection \u2192 user-agent injection.\n6. The response body is deserialized by the runtime codec into the typed `*v1.Pod` Go struct. Non-2xx responses are deserialized into `*errors.StatusError`, which enables programmatic checks like `errors.IsNotFound(err)`.\n\n### 3b. Informer Startup (the canonical controller path)\n\nThis is the flow described in `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/ARCHITECTURE.md` and implemented across `tools/cache/`.\n\n```\nAPI Server\n    \u2502\n    \u2502  LIST (paginated) then WATCH\n    \u25bc\nReflector  (tools/cache/reflector.go)\n    \u2502\n    \u2502  store.Add / store.Update / store.Delete / store.Replace\n    \u25bc\nDeltaFIFO  (tools/cache/delta_fifo.go)\n    \u2502\n    \u2502  Pop() \u2192 HandleDeltas()\n    \u25bc\nsharedIndexInformer (tools/cache/shared_informer.go)\n    \u251c\u2500\u2500 Updates \u2192 Indexer  (tools/cache/thread_safe_store.go)\n    \u2514\u2500\u2500 Notifies \u2192 sharedProcessor \u2192 per-handler listener goroutines\n                                          \u2502\n                                          \u2502  key enqueue\n                                          \u25bc\n                               workqueue.TypedRateLimitingInterface\n                                          \u2502\n                                          \u2502  Get() \u2192 reconcile()\n                                          \u25bc\n                               Controller Business Logic\n                                          \u2502\n                                          \u2502  read from cache via Lister\n                                          \u25bc\n                                       Indexer\n```\n\n**Step 1 \u2013 Reflector initialization** (`reflector.go:RunWithContext`)\n\n`wait.BackoffUntil` drives a loop calling `r.ListAndWatchWithContext(ctx)`. On each iteration:\n- If the `WatchListClient` feature gate is enabled, it first attempts `r.watchList(ctx)` \u2014 a streaming `WATCH` with `sendInitialEvents=true` that delivers the full initial state via watch events instead of a bulk LIST. This reduces memory pressure on the API server.\n- If `WatchListClient` is disabled or the server doesn't support it, `r.list(ctx)` is called. This uses `tools/pager` to issue paginated `LIST` requests with a `limit` parameter, accumulating pages until a `continue` token is empty.\n- After the initial list, `r.watchWithResync(ctx, w)` runs the watch loop.\n\n**Step 2 \u2013 DeltaFIFO as a change buffer** (`delta_fifo.go`)\n\n`DeltaFIFO` is a producer-consumer queue that accumulates `Deltas` (slices of typed changes) for each object key. A key difference from a plain FIFO: multiple rapid changes to the same object are accumulated before consumption, so the controller sees them in sequence without losing intermediate states. The `Replace()` method (called when the LIST completes) synthesizes `Replaced`/`Sync` deltas for all currently-known objects that were not present in the new list (i.e., implicit deletions).\n\n**Step 3 \u2013 sharedIndexInformer as multiplexer** (`shared_informer.go:sharedIndexInformer`)\n\nWhen `RunWithContext` is called, it builds the internal `Config` (connecting `DeltaFIFO`, `ListerWatcher`, and the `HandleDeltas` process function) and starts a `controller`. In a separate goroutine, the `sharedProcessor` fans out notifications to each registered `ResourceEventHandler`. The key insight: multiple independent controllers can register handlers on the same informer, sharing a single LIST/WATCH connection to the API server. The `SharedInformerFactory` (`informers/factory.go`) deduplicates informers by Go type.\n\n**Step 4 \u2013 Handler \u2192 workqueue boundary**\n\nA well-written event handler (e.g., from `AddEventHandler`) should not do reconciliation work. It should only call `queue.Add(key)`. The work queue deduplicates keys: if an object is enqueued multiple times before the controller processes it, only one item is in the queue. This is the primary technique for handling bursty updates cheaply.\n\n**Step 5 \u2013 Controller reads from cache via Lister**\n\nInside the reconcile function, the controller fetches the object's current state from the cache using a generated Lister (e.g., `podLister.Pods(\"default\").Get(\"nginx\")`). This is a pure in-memory read against the `Indexer`'s `ThreadSafeStore`. It does **not** make an API call. Only when the controller needs a guaranteed-fresh view (uncommon) should it call the API directly.\n\n---\n\n## 4. Failure Modes, Limits, and Design Tradeoffs\n\n### Reflector Recovery from API Server Errors\n\nThe reflector's backoff manager (`wait.NewExponentialBackoffManager` in `reflector.go:NewReflectorWithOptions`) uses an 800ms base, 30s max, and 2-minute reset. After a watch disconnects with `io.EOF` or `io.ErrUnexpectedEOF`, the error handler logs at V(1) and the loop retries. After a `410 Gone` (expired resource version), the reflector re-lists from scratch. `isLastSyncResourceVersionUnavailable` tracks this state to avoid passing a stale `resourceVersion` to the relist.\n\nThe watch timeout is randomized between `minWatchTimeout` and `2*minWatchTimeout` (default 5-10 minutes) to spread reconnection load across API server instances.\n\n### DeltaFIFO and \"Too Old\" Resource Version\n\nIf the API server's watch cache has rolled past the client's last-known `resourceVersion`, the server returns a `410 Gone`. The reflector treats this as a non-fatal error and re-lists. Watch bookmarks (empty `BOOKMARK` events carrying a recent `resourceVersion`) mitigate this by periodically advancing the client's `lastSyncResourceVersion` without requiring a full relist.\n\n### Informer Sync vs. Freshness\n\n`HasSynced()` on a `SharedInformer` returns true only after the initial LIST has been processed and all pre-sync notifications have been delivered to every registered handler. Waiting on `WaitForCacheSync` before starting reconciliation is mandatory to avoid acting on a partial cache view. However, `HasSynced() == true` does not mean the cache reflects the current state \u2014 only that it saw at least one complete snapshot. Stale reads are the fundamental trade-off of the cached model.\n\n### Work Queue Rate Limiting\n\n`DefaultTypedControllerRateLimiter` (`util/workqueue/default_rate_limiters.go`) combines two limiters via `MaxOfRateLimiter`:\n- `TypedItemExponentialFailureRateLimiter`: per-item exponential backoff starting at 5ms, capped at 1000s.\n- `TypedBucketRateLimiter`: global token-bucket at 10 QPS with burst of 100.\n\nA controller that forgets to call `queue.Forget(key)` on success will continue to apply exponential backoff to that item indefinitely, causing reconciliation to slow to a crawl over time.\n\n### Client-Side Rate Limiting vs. API Priority and Fairness (APF)\n\n`rest.Config.QPS` and `Burst` enforce a client-side token bucket. This is the client's voluntary contribution to load management. The server's APF system (`flowcontrol.apiserver.k8s.io`) enforces server-side limits independently. When the server returns 429, the `withRetry` logic reads `Retry-After` and waits before the next attempt. The two systems are complementary but not coordinated.\n\n### Shared Informer Memory and CPU Costs\n\nEach `SharedIndexInformer` holds a complete copy of all objects of its type in the `ThreadSafeStore`. For large collections (e.g., all Pods in a large cluster), this is the dominant memory consumer in a controller process. The `TransformFunc` hook (`SetTransform`) allows stripping unneeded fields (e.g., `ManagedFields`, unused annotations) before objects enter the cache, reducing resident memory significantly. This must be set before `Run()` is called.\n\n---\n\n## 5. Extension Points, Customization Hooks, and Risks\n\n### WrapTransport (`rest.Config.WrapTransport`)\n\nInjects a custom `http.RoundTripper` into the transport chain. Used to add request signing, audit logging, or custom metrics. Risk: a faulty wrapper that does not call the inner transport will silently break all API calls.\n\n### TransformFunc (`SharedInformer.SetTransform`)\n\nCalled for each object before it is stored in the cache. The function signature is `func(interface{}) (interface{}, error)`. Can return a different object type entirely. Risk: if you modify the incoming object in place rather than making a copy, you corrupt the Reflector's internal state and break the cache's consistency guarantees. The `MutationDetector` (`tools/cache/mutation_detector.go`) in development mode panics if it detects that a stored object has been mutated after storage.\n\n### WatchErrorHandler (`SharedInformer.SetWatchErrorHandlerWithContext`)\n\nReplaces the default watch error logger. Must be set before `Run()`. Useful for surfacing errors to monitoring systems. Must return quickly; expensive processing must be offloaded.\n\n### Custom Indexers (`SharedIndexInformer.AddIndexers`)\n\n`AddIndexers` must be called before `Run()`. An `IndexFunc` produces one or more indexed string values for a given object, enabling `ByIndex(indexName, value)` lookups with O(1) amortized cost instead of O(n) list scans. Risk: adding indexes after the cache is populated is forbidden by design; calling `AddIndexers` after `Run()` returns an error.\n\n### Custom Resource Event Handlers (`AddEventHandler`)\n\nEvent handler registration returns a `ResourceEventHandlerRegistration` whose `HasSynced()` reports whether the individual handler has seen every item in the initial list. This is more fine-grained than the informer-level `HasSynced()` and is the correct signal to gate reconciliation for a specific handler.\n\n### Feature Gates (`features/`)\n\nClient-side feature gates are defined in `features/known_features.go` and are governed by environment variables (see `features/envvar.go`). Current gates of note:\n- `WatchListClient` (beta, on by default in 1.35+): enables streaming initial state via WATCH semantics rather than paginated LIST. More server-efficient but requires server support.\n- `InOrderInformers` (beta, on by default in 1.33+): ensures watch events are delivered to handlers in order.\n- `InOrderInformersBatchProcess` (beta, on by default in 1.35+): processes batches of delta events atomically, improving throughput.\n- `ClientsAllowCBOR` / `ClientsPreferCBOR` (alpha): opt-in to CBOR wire encoding, which is more compact than JSON for many object types.\n\nOverriding feature gates is done by setting environment variables of the form `KUBE_FEATURE_<FeatureName>=true|false`. This is intended for development and testing, not production configuration.\n\n### Leader Election Callbacks (`tools/leaderelection/`)\n\n`LeaderElectionConfig.Callbacks` accepts `OnStartedLeading`, `OnStoppedLeading`, and `OnNewLeader`. `OnStoppedLeading` is called when the lease is lost. The conventional pattern is to call `os.Exit(1)` in `OnStoppedLeading` to force the process to restart and re-acquire the lock cleanly, rather than continuing in a state where the controller might have stale assumptions. The timing parameters (`LeaseDuration`, `RenewDeadline`, `RetryPeriod`) must satisfy `LeaseDuration > RenewDeadline > RetryPeriod * JitterFactor`; `NewLeaderElector` enforces these constraints.\n\n### Server-Side Apply (`applyconfigurations/`)\n\nThe `applyconfigurations` package is generated code providing type-safe builders for SSA patch objects. The `utils.go` helper provides `ExtractPod`, `ExtractDeployment`, etc., which extract an existing object's apply configuration \u2014 the correct way to compute a desired-state patch from a live object. Using raw JSON patches for SSA is an anti-pattern that loses field ownership information.\n\n---\n\n## 6. Operational Implications\n\n### Memory Pressure from Informer Caches\n\nEach `sharedIndexInformer` stores the full decoded object in memory. In clusters with thousands of Pods or ConfigMaps, the aggregate memory of all informers in a controller process can reach gigabytes. Mitigation strategies, all with code hooks:\n\n1. **`TransformFunc`**: strip `ManagedFields`, `Annotations`, and other large unused fields before storage. Must be set before `Run()` via `factory.WithTransform(fn)` (`informers/factory.go:WithTransform`) or `informer.SetTransform(fn)`.\n2. **Namespace-scoped factories**: `informers/factory.go:WithNamespace` limits the factory to a single namespace, filtering at the API server level via the ListerWatcher's `FieldSelector`.\n3. **`TweakListOptions`** (`informers/factory.go:WithTweakListOptions`): injects custom `ListOptions` (label selectors, field selectors) into the underlying ListerWatcher, narrowing the watched set.\n4. **Metadata-only informers** (`metadata/`): for controllers that only need `ObjectMeta`, using the `MetadataClient` dramatically reduces per-object memory because the spec and status are never decoded.\n\n### Connection Multiplexing\n\nThe `tlsCache` in `transport/transport.go` is a process-wide cache of TLS connections keyed by the transport configuration. This means multiple `Clientset` instances created from equivalent `rest.Config` values will share the same underlying `http.Transport` and its connection pool. Creating many `rest.Config` objects with subtly different settings (e.g., different `UserAgent` values) bypasses this cache and causes TCP connection proliferation.\n\n### Informer Resync and API Server Load\n\nThe `ResyncPeriod` on a `SharedInformerFactory` triggers periodic `Sync` deltas, which re-deliver every object in the cache to all registered handlers. This does **not** contact the API server \u2014 it is a pure in-memory re-notification. The purpose is to let controllers recover from any state they may have missed or from failures in their reconcile logic. A resync period of 10\u201330 minutes is typical. Setting it too short inflates CPU usage in controllers with many watched objects; setting it to zero means controllers only react to real watch events and never self-heal from silent failures.\n\n### Watch Timeout Randomization and API Server Health\n\nThe reflector sets each watch request's `TimeoutSeconds` to a random value in `[minWatchTimeout, 2*minWatchTimeout]` (default: a random value between 5 and 10 minutes). This intentional jitter prevents all informers across a fleet of controllers from reconnecting simultaneously after a scheduled API server restart, which would cause a thundering-herd LIST storm.\n\n### Controller Throughput Tuning\n\nFor controllers processing high volumes of events, `InOrderInformersBatchProcess` (enabled by default at 1.35) batches multiple deltas from the FIFO into a single `ProcessBatch` call. This reduces per-event overhead for the common case where many objects are updated in a short window. The batch size and behavior are controlled by the underlying `QueueWithBatch` interface; the `Config.ProcessBatch` function in `tools/cache/controller.go` is invoked when both the feature gate and the function are non-nil.\n\n---\n\n## 7. File and Directory Map\n\n| Path | Role |\n|------|------|\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/ARCHITECTURE.md` | Official architecture overview from the maintainers |\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/rest/config.go` | `rest.Config`: all connection parameters; `DefaultQPS`/`DefaultBurst` constants |\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/rest/client.go` | `RESTClient`: base HTTP client built from `rest.Config` |\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/rest/request.go` | `Request`: builder pattern for API calls; `MetaNamespaceIndexFunc` |\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/cache/listwatch.go` | `ListerWatcher` interface; `ListerWithContext`/`WatcherWithContext` |\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/cache/mutation_cache.go` | `MutationCache`: LRU overlay for post-write optimistic freshness |\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/util/workqueue/queue.go` | `TypedInterface[T]`: core queue abstraction; deduplication semantics |\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/util/workqueue/delaying_queue.go` | `TypedDelayingInterface[T]`: heap-backed delayed re-enqueue |\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/util/workqueue/rate_limiting_queue.go` | `TypedRateLimitingInterface[T]`: `AddRateLimited`, `Forget` |\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/util/workqueue/default_rate_limiters.go` | `DefaultTypedControllerRateLimiter`: exponential backoff + token bucket combo |\n| `staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/leaderelection/leaderelection.go` | `LeaderElector`: `Lease`-based HA;
